style="font-size:105%;background:transparent;text-align:left">Experiments</div><div class="NavContent plainlist hlist" style="font-size:105%;padding:0.2em 0 0.4em;text-align:center">\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Accelerator_Neutrino_Neutron_Interaction_Experiment" title="Accelerator Neutrino Neutron Interaction Experiment">ANNIE</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Laboratori_Nazionali_del_Gran_Sasso" title="Laboratori Nazionali del Gran Sasso">Gran Sasso</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/India-based_Neutrino_Observatory" title="India-based Neutrino Observatory">INO</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Large_Hadron_Collider" title="Large Hadron Collider">LHC</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Sudbury_Neutrino_Observatory" title="Sudbury Neutrino Observatory">SNO</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Super-Kamiokande" title="Super-Kamiokande">Super-K</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Tevatron" title="Tevatron">Tevatron</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/NOvA" title="NOvA">NOvA</a></li></ul></div></div></td>\n</tr><tr><td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;"><div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Beyond_the_Standard_Model" title="Template:Beyond the Standard Model"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Beyond_the_Standard_Model" title="Template talk:Beyond the Standard Model"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Beyond_the_Standard_Model&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p><b>Dark matter</b> is a <a href="/wiki/Hypothesis" title="Hypothesis">hypothetical</a> form of <a href="/wiki/Matter" title="Matter">matter</a> that is thought to account for approximately 85% of the matter in the <a href="/wiki/Universe" title="Universe">universe</a>, and about a quarter of <a href="/wiki/Universe#Composition" title="Universe">its total energy density</a>. The majority of dark matter is thought to be non-<a href="/wiki/Baryon" title="Baryon">baryonic</a> in nature, possibly being composed of some as-yet undiscovered <a href="/wiki/Subatomic_particle" title="Subatomic particle">subatomic particles</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[note 1]</a></sup> Its presence is implied in a variety of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Astrophysical" title="Astrophysical">astrophysical</a> observations, including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gravitation" title="Gravitation">gravitational</a> effects that cannot be explained unless more matter is present than can be seen. For this reason, most experts think dark matter to be ubiquitous in the universe and to have had a strong influence on its structure and evolution. Dark matter is called dark because it does not appear to interact with observable <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_radiation" title="Electromagnetic radiation">electromagnetic radiation</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Light" title="Light">light</a>, and is thus invisible to the entire <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_spectrum" title="Electromagnetic spectrum">electromagnetic spectrum</a>, making it extremely difficult to detect using usual astronomical equipment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CERN_Dark_Matter_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-CERN_Dark_Matter-2">[1]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The primary evidence for dark matter is that calculations show that many <a href="/wiki/Galaxy" title="Galaxy">galaxies</a> would fly apart instead of rotating, or would not have formed or move as they do, if they did not contain a large amount of unseen matter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Siegfried_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-Siegfried-3">[2]</a></sup> Other lines of evidence include observations in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gravitational_lensing" title="Gravitational lensing">gravitational lensing</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Trimble_1987_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-Trimble_1987-4">[3]</a></sup> from the <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_microwave_background" title="Cosmic microwave background">cosmic microwave background</a>, from astronomical observations of the <a href="/wiki/Observable_universe" title="Observable universe">observable universe</a>\'s current structure, from the <a href="/wiki/Galaxy_formation_and_evolution" title="Galaxy formation and evolution">formation and evolution of galaxies</a>, from mass location during <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Galactic_collision" title="Galactic collision">galactic collisions</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[4]</a></sup> and from the motion of galaxies within <a href="/wiki/Galaxy_cluster" title="Galaxy cluster">galaxy clusters</a>. In the standard <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lambda-CDM" title="Lambda-CDM">Lambda-CDM</a> model of cosmology, the total <a href="/wiki/Mass%E2%80%93energy_equivalence" title="Mass&#8211;energy equivalence">mass&#8211;energy</a> of the universe contains 5% <a href="/wiki/Baryon#Baryonic_matter" title="Baryon">ordinary</a> <a href="/wiki/Matter" title="Matter">matter</a> and <a href="/wiki/Energy" title="Energy">energy</a>, 27% dark matter and 68% of an unknown form of energy known as <a href="/wiki/Dark_energy" title="Dark energy">dark energy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA_Planck_Mission_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA_Planck_Mission-6">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA_Science_Dark_Matter_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA_Science_Dark_Matter-7">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-planck_overview_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-planck_overview-8">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wmap7parameters(a)_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-wmap7parameters(a)-9">[8]</a></sup> Thus, dark matter constitutes 85%<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[note 2]</a></sup> of total <a href="/wiki/Mass" title="Mass">mass</a>, while dark energy plus dark matter constitute 95% of total mass&#8211;energy content.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-planckcam_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-planckcam-11">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DarkMatter_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-DarkMatter-12">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wmap7parameters_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-wmap7parameters-14">[12]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Because dark matter has not yet been observed directly, if it exists, it must barely interact with ordinary <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Baryonic" title="Baryonic">baryonic</a> matter and radiation, except through gravity. The primary candidate for dark matter is some new kind of <a href="/wiki/Elementary_particle" title="Elementary particle">elementary particle</a> that has <a href="/wiki/Physics_beyond_the_Standard_Model" title="Physics beyond the Standard Model">not yet been discovered</a>, in particular, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Weakly-interacting_massive_particles" title="Weakly-interacting massive particles">weakly-interacting massive particles</a> (WIMPs), or <a href="/wiki/Gravitationally-interacting_massive_particles" title="Gravitationally-interacting massive particles">gravitationally-interacting massive particles</a> (GIMPs).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Copi_1995_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-Copi_1995-15">[13]</a></sup> Many experiments to directly detect and study dark matter particles are being actively undertaken, but none has yet succeeded.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bertone_hooper_silk_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-bertone_hooper_silk-16">[14]</a></sup> Dark matter is classified as cold, warm, or hot according to its <a href="/wiki/Velocity" title="Velocity">velocity</a> (more precisely, its <a href="/wiki/Free_streaming" title="Free streaming">free streaming length</a>). Current models favor a <a href="/wiki/Cold_dark_matter" title="Cold dark matter">cold dark matter</a> scenario, in which <a href="/wiki/Structure_formation" title="Structure formation">structures emerge</a> by gradual accumulation of particles.\n</p><p>Although the existence of dark matter is generally accepted by the scientific community, some astrophysicists,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[15]</a></sup> intrigued by certain observations that do not fit the dark matter theory,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-physicsworldcorrelation_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-physicsworldcorrelation-18">[16]</a></sup> argue for various modifications of the standard laws of <a href="/wiki/General_relativity" title="General relativity">general relativity</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Modified_Newtonian_dynamics" title="Modified Newtonian dynamics">modified Newtonian dynamics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tensor%E2%80%93vector%E2%80%93scalar_gravity" title="Tensor&#8211;vector&#8211;scalar gravity">tensor&#8211;vector&#8211;scalar gravity</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Entropic_gravity" title="Entropic gravity">entropic gravity</a>. These models attempt to account for all observations without invoking supplemental non-baryonic matter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Angus_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-Angus-19">[17]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r872693506">.mw-parser-output .toclimit-2 .toclevel-1 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-3 .toclevel-2 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-4 .toclevel-3 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-5 .toclevel-4 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-6 .toclevel-5 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-7 .toclevel-6 ul{display:none}</style><div class="toclimit-3"><div class="toc" id="toc"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" dir="ltr" lang="en"><h2>Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-1 definition</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-5"><a href="#Observational_evidence"><span class="tocnumber">3</span> <span class="toctext">Observational evidence</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-6"><a href="#Galaxy_rotation_curves"><span class="tocnumber">3.1</span> <span class="toctext">Galaxy rotation curves</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 Cluster</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-13"><a href="#Type_Ia_supernova_distance_measurements"><span class="tocnumber">3.8</span> <span class="toctext">Type Ia supernova distance measurements</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-14"><a href="#Sky_surveys_and_baryon_acoustic_oscillations"><span class="tocnumber">3.9</span> <span class="toctext">Sky surveys and baryon acoustic oscillations</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-15"><a href="#Redshift-space_distortions"><span class="tocnumber">3.10</span> <span class="toctext">Redshift-space forest</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-17"><a href="#Composition_of_dark_matter:_baryonic_vs._nonbaryonic"><span class="tocnumber">4</span> <span class="toctext">Composition of dark matter: baryonic vs. nonbaryonic</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-18"><a href="#Baryonic_matter"><span class="tocnumber">4.1</span> <span class="toctext">Baryonic matter</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-19"><a href="#Non-baryonic_matter"><span class="tocnumber">4.2</span> <span class="toctext">Non-baryonic matter</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-20"><a href="#Dark_matter_aggregation_and_dense_dark_matter_objects"><span class="tocnumber">4.3</span> <span class="toctext">Dark matter aggregation and dense dark matter objects</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-21"><a href="#Classification_of_dark_matter:_cold,_warm_or_hot"><span class="tocnumber">5</span> <span class="toctext">Classification of dark matter: cold, warm or hot</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-22"><a href="#Alternative_definitions"><span class="tocnumber">5.1</span> <span class="toctext">Alternative definitions</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-23"><a href="#Cold_dark_matter"><span class="tocnumber">5.2</span> <span class="toctext">Cold dark matter</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-24"><a href="#Warm_dark_matter"><span class="tocnumber">5.3</span> <span class="toctext">Warm dark matter</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-25"><a href="#Hot_dark_matter"><span class="tocnumber">5.4</span> <span class="toctext">Hot dark matter</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-26"><a href="#Detection_of_dark_matter_particles"><span class="tocnumber">6</span> <span class="toctext">Detection of dark matter particles</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-27"><a href="#Direct_detection"><span class="tocnumber">6.1</span> <span class="toctext">Direct detection</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-28"><a tocsection-30"><a href="#Alternative_hypotheses"><span class="tocnumber">7</span> <span class="toctext">Alternative hypotheses</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-31"><a href="#In_philosophy_of_science"><span class="tocnumber">8</span> <span class="toctext">In philosophy of science</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-32"><a href="#In_popular_culture"><span class="tocnumber">9</span> <span class="toctext">In popular culture</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-33"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">10</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-34"><a href="#Notes"><span class="tocnumber">11</span> <span class="toclevel-2 tocsection-36"><a href="#Bibliography"><span class="tocnumber">12.1</span> <span class="toctext">Bibliography</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-37"><a href="#Further_reading"><span class="tocnumber">13</span> <span class="toctext">Further reading</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-38"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">14</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n</div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dark_matter&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<table class="box-Refimprove_science plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Primary_sources" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div style="width:52px"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg"><img alt="Question book-new.svg" data-file-height="399" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="39" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" width="50"/></a></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs additional <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Scientific_citation_guidelines" title="Wikipedia:Scientific citation guidelines">citations</a> to <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Reliable_sources#Scholarship" title="Wikipedia:Reliable sources">secondary or tertiary sources</a></b><span class="hide-when-compact"> such as review articles, monographs, or textbooks.  Please add such references to provide context and establish the relevance of any <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:No_original_research#Primary,_secondary_and_tertiary_sources" title="Wikipedia:No original research">primary research articles</a> cited. Unsourced or poorly sourced material may be challenged and removed.</span> <small class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">December 2016</span>)</i></small><small class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_history">Early history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dark_matter&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Early history">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The hypothesis of dark matter has an elaborate history.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[18]</a></sup> In a talk given in 1884,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[19]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/William_Thomson,_1st_Baron_Kelvin" title="William Thomson, 1st Baron Kelvin">Lord Kelvin</a> estimated the number of dark bodies in the <a href="/wiki/Milky_Way" title="Milky Way">Milky Way</a> from the observed velocity dispersion of the stars orbiting around the center of the galaxy. By using these measurements, he estimated the mass of the galaxy, which he determined is different from the mass of visible stars. Lord Kelvin thus concluded that "many of our stars, perhaps a great majority of them, may be dark bodies".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-22">[20]</a></sup> In 1906 <a href="/wiki/Henri_Poincar%C3%A9" title="Henri Poincar&#233;">Henri Poincar&#233;</a> in "The Milky Way and Theory of Gases" used "dark matter", or "mati&#232;re obscure" in French, in discussing Kelvin\'s work.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_22-1"><a href="#cite_note-:1-22">[20]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The first to suggest the existence of dark matter, using stellar velocities, was Dutch astronomer <a href="/wiki/Jacobus_Kapteyn" title="Jacobus Kapteyn">Jacobus Kapteyn</a> in 1922.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Patras2014_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-Patras2014-24">[22]</a></sup> Fellow Dutchman and radio astronomy pioneer <a href="/wiki/Jan_Oort" title="Jan Oort">Jan Oort</a> also hypothesized the existence of dark matter in 1932.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Patras2014_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-Patras2014-24">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[24]</a></sup> Oort was studying stellar motions in the <a href="/wiki/Local_Group" title="Local Group">local galactic neighborhood</a> and found that the mass in the galactic plane must be greater than what was observed, but this measurement was later determined to be erroneous.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[25]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1933, Swiss astrophysicist <a href="/wiki/Fritz_Zwicky" title="Fritz Zwicky">Fritz Zwicky</a>, who studied <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Groups_and_clusters_of_galaxies" title="Groups and clusters of galaxies">galaxy clusters</a> while working at the California Institute of Technology, made a similar inference.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[28]</a></sup> Zwicky applied the <a href="/wiki/Virial_theorem" title="Virial theorem">virial theorem</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Coma_Cluster" title="Coma Cluster">Coma Cluster</a> and obtained evidence of unseen mass that he called <i>dunkle Materie</i> (\'dark matter\'). Zwicky estimated its mass based on the motions of galaxies near its edge and compared that to an estimate based on its brightness and number of galaxies. He estimated that the cluster had about 400 times more mass than was visually observable. The gravity effect of the visible galaxies was far too small for such fast orbits, thus mass must be hidden from view. Based on these conclusions, Zwicky inferred that some unseen matter provided the mass and associated gravitation attraction to hold the cluster together. This was the first formal inference about the existence of dark matter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-31">[29]</a></sup> Zwicky\'s estimates were off by more than an order of magnitude, mainly due to an obsolete value of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hubble_constant" title="Hubble constant">Hubble constant</a>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[30]</a></sup> the same calculation today shows a smaller fraction, using greater values for luminous mass. However, Zwicky did correctly infer that the bulk of the matter was dark.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (August 2016)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_31-1"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-31">[29]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Further indications that the <a href="/wiki/Mass-to-light_ratio" title="Mass-to-light ratio">mass-to-light ratio</a> was not unity came from measurements of galaxy rotation curves. In 1939, <a href="/wiki/Horace_W._Babcock" title="Horace W. Babcock">Horace W. Babcock</a> reported the rotation curve for the <a href="/wiki/Andromeda_Galaxy" title="Andromeda Galaxy">Andromeda nebula</a> (known now as the Andromeda Galaxy), which suggested that the mass-to-luminosity ratio increases radially.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-33">[31]</a></sup> He attributed it to either light absorption within the galaxy or modified dynamics in the outer portions of the spiral and not to the missing matter that he had uncovered. Following <a href="/wiki/Horace_W._Babcock" title="Horace W. Babcock">Babcock\'s</a> 1939 report of unexpectedly rapid rotation in the outskirts of the Andromeda galaxy and a mass-to-light ratio of 50, in 1940 <a href="/wiki/Jan_Oort" title="Jan Oort">Jan Oort</a> discovered and wrote about the large\nnon-visible halo of NGC 3115.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[32]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1970s">1970s</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dark_matter&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: 1970s">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Vera_Rubin" title="Vera Rubin">Vera Rubin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kent_Ford_(astronomer)" title="Kent Ford (astronomer)">Kent Ford</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ken_Freeman_(astronomer)" title="Ken Freeman (astronomer)">Ken Freeman\'s</a> work in the 1960s and 1970s,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[33]</a></sup> provided further strong evidence, also using galaxy rotation curves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20161227_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20161227-36">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rubin1970_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rubin1970-38">[36]</a></sup> Rubin worked with a new <a href="/wiki/Spectrograph" title="Spectrograph">spectrograph</a> to measure the <a href="/wiki/Galaxy_rotation_curve" title="Galaxy rotation curve">velocity curve</a> of edge-on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spiral_galaxies" title="Spiral galaxies">spiral galaxies</a> with greater accuracy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rubin1970_38-1"><a href="#cite_note-Rubin1970-38">[36]</a></sup> This result was confirmed in 1978.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[37]</a></sup> An influential paper presented Rubin\'s results in 1980.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rubin1980_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rubin1980-40">[38]</a></sup> Rubin found that most galaxies must contain about six times as much dark as visible mass;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERandall201513&#8211;14_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERandall201513&#8211;14-41">[39]</a></sup> thus, by around 1980 the apparent need for dark matter was widely recognized as a major unsolved problem in astronomy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20161227_36-1"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20161227-36">[34]</a></sup>\n</p><p>At the same time that Rubin and Ford were exploring optical rotation curves, radio astronomers were making use of new radio telescopes to map the 21&#160;cm line of atomic hydrogen in nearby galaxies. The radial distribution of interstellar atomic hydrogen (<a href="/wiki/H_I_region" title="H I region">HI</a>) often extends to much larger galactic radii than those accessible by optical studies, extending the sampling of rotation curves&#8212;and thus of the total mass distribution&#8212;to a new dynamical regime. Early mapping of Andromeda with the 300-foot telescope at Green Bank<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Roberts1966_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-Roberts1966-42">[40]</a></sup> and the 250-foot dish at Jodrell Bank<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gottesman1966_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gottesman1966-43">[41]</a></sup> already showed that the HI rotation curve did not trace the expected Keplerian decline. As more sensitive receivers became available, Morton Roberts and Robert Whitehurst<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Roberts1975_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-Roberts1975-44">[42]</a></sup> were able to trace the rotational velocity of Andromeda to 30 kpc, much beyond the optical measurements. Illustrating the advantage of tracing the gas disk at large radii, Figure 16 of that paper<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Roberts1975_44-1"><a href="#cite_note-Roberts1975-44">[42]</a></sup> combines the optical data<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rubin1970_38-2"><a href="#cite_note-Rubin1970-38">[36]</a></sup> (the cluster of points at radii of less than 15 kpc with a single point further out) with the HI data between 20 and 30 kpc, exhibiting the flatness of the outer galaxy rotation curve; the solid curve peaking at the center is the optical surface density, while the other curve shows the cumulative mass, still rising linearly at the outermost measurement. In parallel, the use of interferometric arrays for extragalactic HI spectroscopy was being developed. In 1972, David Rogstad and Seth Shostak<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rogstad1972_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rogstad1972-45">[43]</a></sup> published HI rotation curves of five spirals mapped with the Owens Valley interferometer; the rotation curves of all five were very flat, suggesting very large values of mass-to-light ratio in the outer parts of their extended HI disks.\n</p><p>A stream of observations in the 1980s supported the presence of dark matter, including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gravitational_lensing" title="Gravitational lensing">gravitational lensing</a> of background objects by <a href="/wiki/Galaxy_cluster" title="Galaxy cluster">galaxy clusters</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERandall201514&#8211;16_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERandall201514&#8211;16-46">[44]</a></sup> the temperature distribution of hot gas in galaxies and clusters, and the pattern of anisotropies in the <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_microwave_background" title="Cosmic microwave background">cosmic microwave background</a>. According to consensus among cosmologists, dark matter is composed primarily of a not yet characterized type of <a href="/wiki/Subatomic_particle" title="Subatomic particle">subatomic particle</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Copi_1995_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-Copi_1995-15">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bergstrom_2000_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bergstrom_2000-47">[45]</a></sup> The search for this particle, by a variety of means, is one of the major efforts in <a href="/wiki/Particle_physics" title="Particle physics">particle physics</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bertone_hooper_silk_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-bertone_hooper_silk-16">[14]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Technical_definition">Technical definition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dark_matter&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Technical definition">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Friedmann_equations" title="Friedmann equations">Friedmann equations</a></div>\n<p>In standard cosmology, matter is anything whose energy density scales with the inverse cube of the <a href="/wiki/Scale_factor_(cosmology)" title="Scale factor (cosmology)">scale factor</a>, i.e., <i>&#961; &#8733; a<sup>&#8722;3</sup></i>. This is in contrast to radiation, which scales as the inverse fourth power of the scale factor <i> &#961; &#8733; a<sup>&#8722;4</sup> </i>, and a <a href="/wiki/Cosmological_constant" title="Cosmological constant">cosmological constant</a>, which is independent of <i>a</i>. These scalings can be understood intuitively: for an ordinary particle in a cubical box, doubling the length of the sides of the box decreases the density (and hence energy density) by a factor of eight (<i>2<sup>3</sup></i>). For radiation, the decrease in energy density is larger because an increase in scale factor causes a proportional <a href="/wiki/Redshift" title="Redshift">redshift</a>. A cosmological constant, as an intrinsic property of space, has a constant energy density regardless of the volume under consideration.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[47]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In principle, "dark matter" means all components of the universe that are not visible but still obey <i>&#961; &#8733; a<sup>&#8722;3</sup></i>. In practice, the term "dark matter" is often used to mean only the non-baryonic component of dark matter, i.e., excluding "<a href="/wiki/Missing_baryon_problem" title="Missing baryon problem">missing baryons</a>." Context will usually indicate which meaning is intended.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Observational_evidence">Observational evidence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span codecs=&amp;quot;vp9, opus&amp;quot;" data-title="VP9 (360P)" data-shorttitle="VP9 360P" data-transcodekey="360p.vp9.webm" data-width="640" data-height="360" data-bandwidth="632144" data-framerate="25"/&gt;&lt;/video&gt;&lt;/div&gt;\'><img alt="File:Comparison of rotating disc galaxies in the distant Universe and the present day.webm" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Comparison_of_rotating_disc_galaxies_in_the_distant_Universe_and_the_present_day.webm/220px--Comparison_of_rotating_disc_galaxies_in_the_distant_Universe_and_the_present_day.webm.jpg" style="width:220px;height:124px"/><a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/4/4c/Comparison_of_rotating_disc_galaxies_in_the_distant_Universe_and_the_present_day.webm" target="new" title="Play media"><span class="play-btn-large"><span class="mw-tmh-playtext">Play media</span></span></a></div> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Comparison_of_rotating_disc_galaxies_in_the_distant_Universe_and_the_present_day.webm" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Models of rotating disc galaxies in the present day (left) and ten billion years ago (right). In the present-day galaxy, dark matter&#8212;shown in red&#8212;is more concentrated near the center and it rotates more rapidly (effect exaggerated).</div></div></div>\n<p>The arms of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spiral_galaxies" title="Spiral galaxies">spiral galaxies</a> rotate around the galactic center. The luminous mass density of a spiral galaxy decreases as one goes from the center to the outskirts. If luminous mass were all the matter, then we can model the galaxy as a point mass in the centre and test masses orbiting around it, similar to the <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">Solar System</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[note 3]</a></sup> From <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kepler%27s_Second_Law" title="Kepler\'s Second Law">Kepler\'s Second Law</a>, it is expected that the rotation velocities will decrease with distance from the center, similar to the Solar System. This is not observed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[49]</a></sup> Instead, the galaxy rotation curve remains flat as distance from the center increases.\n</p><p>If Kepler\'s laws are correct, then the obvious way to resolve this discrepancy is to conclude that the mass distribution in spiral galaxies is not similar to that of the Solar System. In particular, there is a lot of non-luminous matter (dark matter) in the outskirts of the galaxy.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Velocity_dispersions">Velocity dispersions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dark_matter&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Velocity dispersions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Velocity_dispersion" title="Velocity dispersion">Velocity dispersion</a></div>\n<p>Stars in bound systems must obey the <a href="/wiki/Virial_theorem" title="Virial theorem">virial theorem</a>. The theorem, together with the measured velocity distribution, can be used to measure the mass distribution in a bound system, such as elliptical galaxies or globular clusters. With some exceptions, velocity dispersion estimates of elliptical galaxies<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[50]</a></sup> do not match the predicted velocity dispersion from the observed mass distribution, even assuming complicated distributions of stellar orbits.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[51]</a></sup>\n</p><p>As with galaxy rotation curves, the obvious way to resolve the discrepancy is to postulate the existence of non-luminous matter.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Galaxy_clusters">Galaxy clusters</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dark_matter&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Galaxy clusters">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Galaxy_clusters" title="Galaxy clusters">Galaxy clusters</a> are particularly important for dark matter studies since their masses can be estimated in three independent ways:\n</p>\n<ul><li>From the scatter in radial velocities of the galaxies within clusters</li>\n<li>From <a href="/wiki/X-ray" title="X-ray">X-rays</a> emitted by hot gas in the clusters. From the X-ray energy spectrum and flux, the gas temperature and density can be estimated, hence giving the pressure; assuming pressure and gravity balance determines the cluster\'s mass profile.</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Gravitational_lens" title="Gravitational lens">Gravitational lensing</a> (usually of more distant galaxies) can measure cluster masses without relying on observations of dynamics (e.g., velocity).</li></ul>\n<p>Generally, these three methods are in reasonable agreement that dark matter outweighs visible matter by approximately 5 to 1.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[52]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Gravitational_lensing">Gravitational lensing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dark_matter&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Gravitational lensing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gravitationell-lins-4.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4000" data-file-width="3853" decoding="async" height="176" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Gravitationell-lins-4.jpg/170px-Gravitationell-lins-4.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Gravitationell-lins-4.jpg/255px-Gravitationell-lins-4.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Gravitationell-lins-4.jpg/340px-Gravitationell-lins-4.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Gravitationell-lins-4.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Strong gravitational lensing as observed by the <a href="/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope" title="Hubble Space Telescope">Hubble Space Telescope</a> in <a href="/wiki/Abell_1689" title="Abell 1689">Abell 1689</a> indicates the presence of dark matter&#8212;enlarge the image to see the lensing arcs.</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dark_matter_map_of_KiDS_survey_region_(region_G12).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="472" data-file-width="1280" decoding="async" height="81" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Dark_matter_map_of_KiDS_survey_region_%28region_G12%29.jpg/220px-Dark_matter_map_of_KiDS_survey_region_%28region_G12%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Dark_matter_map_of_KiDS_survey_region_%28region_G12%29.jpg/330px-Dark_matter_map_of_KiDS_survey_region_%28region_G12%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Dark_matter_map_of_KiDS_survey_region_%28region_G12%29.jpg/440px-Dark_matter_map_of_KiDS_survey_region_%28region_G12%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dark_matter_map_of_KiDS_survey_region_(region_G12).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Dark matter map for a patch of sky based on gravitational lensing analysis of a Kilo-Degree survey.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[53]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>One of the consequences of <a href="/wiki/General_relativity" title="General relativity">general relativity</a> is that massive objects (such as a <a href="/wiki/Galaxy_cluster" title="Galaxy cluster">cluster of galaxies</a>) lying between a more distant source (such as a <a href="/wiki/Quasar" title="Quasar">quasar</a>) and an observer should act as a lens to bend the light from this source. The more massive an object, the more lensing is observed.\n</p><p>Strong lensing is the observed distortion of background galaxies into arcs when their light passes through such a gravitational lens. It has been observed around many distant clusters including <a href="/wiki/Abell_1689" title="Abell 1689">Abell 1689</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[54]</a></sup> By measuring the distortion geometry, the mass of the intervening cluster can be obtained. In the dozens of cases where this has been done, the mass-to-light ratios obtained correspond to the dynamical dark matter measurements of clusters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[55]</a></sup> Lensing can lead to multiple copies of an image. By analyzing the distribution of multiple image copies, scientists have been able to deduce and map the distribution of dark matter around the <a href="/wiki/MACS_J0416.1-2403" title="MACS J0416.1-2403">MACS J0416.1-2403</a> galaxy cluster.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[56]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[57]</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Weak_gravitational_lensing" title="Weak gravitational lensing">Weak gravitational lensing</a> investigates minute distortions of galaxies, using statistical analyses from vast <a href="/wiki/Redshift_survey" title="Redshift survey">galaxy surveys</a>. By examining the apparent shear deformation of the adjacent background galaxies, the mean distribution of dark matter can be characterized. The mass-to-light ratios correspond to dark matter densities predicted by other large-scale structure measurements.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[58]</a></sup> Dark matter does not bend light itself; mass (in this case the mass of the dark matter) bends <a href="/wiki/Spacetime" title="Spacetime">spacetime</a>. Light follows the curvature of spacetime, resulting in the lensing effect.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[59]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[60]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cosmic_microwave_background">Cosmic microwave background</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dark_matter&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Cosmic microwave background">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_microwave_background" title="Cosmic microwave background">Cosmic microwave background</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:WMAP_2008_universe_content.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3900" data-file-width="2763" decoding="async" height="311" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/WMAP_2008_universe_content.png/220px-WMAP_2008_universe_content.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/WMAP_2008_universe_content.png/330px-WMAP_2008_universe_content.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/WMAP_2008_universe_content.png/440px-WMAP_2008_universe_content.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:WMAP_2008_universe_content.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Estimated division of total energy in the universe into matter, dark matter and dark energy based on five years of WMAP data.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[61]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>Although both dark matter and ordinary matter are matter, they do not behave in the same way. In particular, in the early universe, ordinary matter was ionized and interacted strongly with radiation via <a href="/wiki/Thomson_scattering" title="Thomson scattering">Thomson scattering</a>. Dark matter does not interact directly with radiation, but it does affect the CMB by its gravitational potential (mainly on large scales), and by its effects on the density and velocity of ordinary matter. Ordinary and dark matter perturbations, therefore, evolve differently with time and leave different imprints on the cosmic microwave background (CMB).\n</p><p>The cosmic microwave background is very close to a perfect blackbody but contains very small temperature anisotropies of a few parts in 100,000. A sky map of anisotropies can be decomposed into an angular power spectrum, which is observed to contain a series of acoustic peaks at near-equal spacing but different heights.\nThe series of peaks can be predicted for any assumed set of cosmological parameters by modern computer codes such as CMBFast and CAMB, and matching theory to data, therefore, constrains cosmological parameters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wayne_Hu_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wayne_Hu-65">[62]</a></sup> The first peak mostly shows the density of baryonic matter, while the third peak relates mostly to the density of dark matter, measuring the density of matter and the density of atoms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wayne_Hu_65-1"><a href="#cite_note-Wayne_Hu-65">[62]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The CMB anisotropy was first discovered by <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_Background_Explorer" title="Cosmic Background Explorer">COBE</a> in 1992, though this had too coarse resolution to detect the acoustic peaks.\nAfter the discovery of the first acoustic peak by the balloon-borne <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/BOOMERanG" title="BOOMERanG">BOOMERanG</a> experiment in 2000,\nthe power spectrum was precisely observed by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/WMAP" title="WMAP">WMAP</a> in 2003&#8211;12, and even more precisely\nby the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Planck_spacecraft" title="Planck spacecraft">Planck spacecraft</a> in 2013&#8211;15. The results support the Lambda-CDM model.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hinshaw2009_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hinshaw2009-66">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Planck15_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-Planck15-67">[64]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The observed CMB angular power spectrum provides powerful evidence in support of dark matter, as its precise structure is well fitted by the <a href="/wiki/Lambda-CDM_model" title="Lambda-CDM model">Lambda-CDM model</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Planck15_67-1"><a href="#cite_note-Planck15-67">[64]</a></sup> but difficult to reproduce with any competing model such as <a href="/wiki/Modified_Newtonian_dynamics" title="Modified Newtonian dynamics">modified Newtonian dynamics</a> (MOND).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Planck15_67-2"><a href="#cite_note-Planck15-67">[64]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Structure_formation">Structure formation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dark_matter&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Structure formation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Structure_formation" title="Structure formation">Structure formation</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:COSMOS_3D_dark_matter_map.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2146" data-file-width="2823" decoding="async" height="167" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/COSMOS_3D_dark_matter_map.png/220px-COSMOS_3D_dark_matter_map.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/COSMOS_3D_dark_matter_map.png/330px-COSMOS_3D_dark_matter_map.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/COSMOS_3D_dark_matter_map.png/440px-COSMOS_3D_dark_matter_map.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:COSMOS_3D_dark_matter_map.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>3D map of the large-scale distribution of dark matter, reconstructed from measurements of <a href="/wiki/Weak_gravitational_lensing" title="Weak gravitational lensing">weak gravitational lensing</a> with the <a href="/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope" title="Hubble Space Telescope">Hubble Space Telescope</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[66]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>Structure formation refers to the period after the Big Bang when density perturbations collapsed to form stars, galaxies, and clusters. Prior to structure formation, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/FRW_metric" title="FRW metric">Friedmann solutions</a> to general relativity describe a homogeneous universe. Later, small anisotropies gradually grew and condensed the homogeneous universe into stars, galaxies and larger structures. Ordinary matter is affected by radiation, which is the dominant element of the universe at very early times. As a result, its density perturbations are washed out and unable to condense into structure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jaffe_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jaffe-70">[67]</a></sup> If there were only ordinary matter in the universe, there would not have been enough time for density perturbations to grow into the galaxies and clusters currently seen.\n</p><p>Dark matter provides a solution to this problem because it is unaffected by radiation. Therefore, its density perturbations can grow first. The resulting gravitational potential acts as an attractive <a href="/wiki/Potential_well" title="Potential well">potential well</a> for ordinary matter collapsing later, speeding up the structure formation process.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jaffe_70-1"><a href="#cite_note-Jaffe-70">[67]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[68]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Bullet_Cluster">Bullet Cluster</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dark_matter&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Bullet Cluster">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Bullet_Cluster" title="Bullet Cluster">Bullet Cluster</a></div>\n<p>If dark matter does not exist, then the next most likely explanation is that general relativity&#8212;the prevailing theory of gravity&#8212;is incorrect and must be modified. The Bullet Cluster, the result of a recent collision of two galaxy clusters, provides a challenge for modified gravity theories because its apparent center of mass is far displaced from the baryonic center of mass.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[69]</a></sup> Standard dark matter theory can easily explain this observation, but modified gravity has a much harder time,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[70]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[71]</a></sup> especially since the observational evidence is model-independent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[72]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Type_Ia_supernova_distance_measurements">Type Ia supernova distance measurements</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dark_matter&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Type Ia supernova distance measurements">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Type_Ia_supernova" title="Type Ia supernova">Type Ia supernova</a> and <a href="/wiki/Shape_of_the_universe" title="Shape of the universe">Shape of the universe</a></div>\n<p>Type Ia <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Supernovae" title="Supernovae">supernovae</a> can be used as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Standard_candles" title="Standard candles">standard candles</a> to measure extragalactic distances, which can in turn be used to measure how fast the universe has expanded in the past. The data indicates that the universe is expanding at an accelerating rate, the cause of which is usually ascribed to <a href="/wiki/Dark_energy" title="Dark energy">dark energy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[73]</a></sup> Since observations indicate the universe is almost flat,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA_Shape_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA_Shape-77">[74]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fermi_Flat_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fermi_Flat-78">[75]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[76]</a></sup> it is expected that the total energy density of everything in the universe should sum to 1 (&#937;<sub>tot</sub> ~ 1). The measured dark energy density is &#937;<sub>&#923;</sub> = ~0.690; the observed ordinary (baryonic) matter energy density is &#937;<sub>b</sub> = ~0.0482 and the energy density of radiation is negligible. This leaves a missing &#937;<sub>dm</sub> = ~0.258 that nonetheless behaves like matter (see technical definition section above)&#8212;dark matter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-planckesa2015_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-planckesa2015-80">[77]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sky_surveys_and_baryon_acoustic_oscillations">Sky surveys and baryon acoustic oscillations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dark_matter&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Sky surveys and baryon acoustic oscillations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Baryon_acoustic_oscillations" title="Baryon acoustic oscillations">Baryon acoustic oscillations</a></div>\n<p>Baryon acoustic oscillations (BAO) are fluctuations in the density of the visible <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Baryonic" title="Baryonic">baryonic</a> matter (normal matter) of the universe on large scales. These are predicted to arise in the Lambda-CDM model due to acoustic oscillations in the photon-baryon fluid of the early universe, and can be observed in the cosmic microwave background angular power spectrum. BAOs set up a preferred length scale for baryons. As the dark matter and baryons clumped together after recombination, the effect is much weaker in the galaxy distribution in the nearby universe, but is detectable as a subtle (~ 1 percent) preference for pairs of galaxies to be separated by 147 Mpc, compared to those separated by 130 or 160 Mpc. This feature was predicted theoretically in the 1990s and then discovered in 2005, in two large galaxy redshift surveys, the <a href="/wiki/Sloan_Digital_Sky_Survey" title="Sloan Digital Sky Survey">Sloan Digital Sky Survey</a> and the <a href="/wiki/2dF_Galaxy_Redshift_Survey" title="2dF Galaxy Redshift Survey">2dF Galaxy Redshift Survey</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[78]</a></sup> Combining the CMB observations with BAO measurements from galaxy <a href="/wiki/Redshift_survey" title="Redshift survey">redshift surveys</a> provides a precise estimate of the <a href="/wiki/Hubble%27s_law" title="Hubble\'s law">Hubble constant</a> and the average matter density in the Universe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Komatsu2009_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-Komatsu2009-82">[79]</a></sup> The results support the Lambda-CDM model.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Redshift-space_distortions">Redshift-space distortions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dark_matter&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Redshift-space distortions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Large galaxy <a href="/wiki/Redshift_survey" title="Redshift survey">redshift surveys</a> may be used to make a three-dimensional map of the galaxy distribution. These maps are slightly distorted because distances are estimated from observed <a href="/wiki/Redshift" title="Redshift">redshifts</a>; the redshift contains a contribution from the galaxy\'s so-called peculiar velocity in addition to the dominant Hubble expansion term. On average, superclusters are expanding but more slowly than the cosmic mean due to their gravity, while voids are expanding faster than average. In a redshift map, galaxies in front of a supercluster have excess radial velocities towards it and have redshifts slightly higher than their distance would imply, while galaxies behind the supercluster have redshifts slightly low for their distance. This effect causes superclusters to appear squashed in the radial direction, and likewise voids are stretched. Their angular positions are unaffected.\nThe effect is not detectable for any one structure since the true shape is not known, but can be measured by averaging over many structures assuming Earth is not at a special location in the Universe.\n</p><p>The effect was predicted quantitatively by Nick Kaiser in 1987, and first decisively measured in 2001 by the <a href="/wiki/2dF_Galaxy_Redshift_Survey" title="2dF Galaxy Redshift Survey">2dF Galaxy Redshift Survey</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[80]</a></sup> Results are in agreement with the <a href="/wiki/Lambda-CDM_model" title="Lambda-CDM model">Lambda-CDM model</a>.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Lyman-alpha_forest">Lyman-alpha forest</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dark_matter&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Lyman-alpha forest">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Lyman-alpha_forest" title="Lyman-alpha forest">Lyman-alpha forest</a></div>\n<p>In <a href="/wiki/Astronomical_spectroscopy" title="Astronomical spectroscopy">astronomical spectroscopy</a>, the Lyman-alpha forest is the sum of the <a href="/wiki/Spectral_line" title="Spectral line">absorption lines</a> arising from the <a href="/wiki/Lyman_series" title="Lyman series">Lyman-alpha</a> transition of <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_line" title="Hydrogen line">neutral hydrogen</a> in the spectra of distant <a href="/wiki/Galaxy" title="Galaxy">galaxies</a> and <a href="/wiki/Quasar" title="Quasar">quasars</a>. Lyman-alpha forest observations can also constrain cosmological models.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[81]</a></sup> These constraints agree with those obtained from WMAP data.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Composition_of_dark_matter:_baryonic_vs._nonbaryonic">Composition of dark matter: baryonic vs. nonbaryonic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dark_matter&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Composition of dark matter: baryonic vs. nonbaryonic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>There are various <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hypotheses" title="Hypotheses">hypotheses</a> about what dark matter could consist of, as set out in the table below.\n</p>\n<table style="float:right; width:30%; margin:0em 1em 1em 1em; background:#eee; border:#ccc solid">\n<tbody><tr>\n<td><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Question_dropshade.png"><img alt="Question dropshade.png" data-file-height="50" data-file-width="50" decoding="async" height="20" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Question_dropshade.png/20px-Question_dropshade.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Question_dropshade.png/30px-Question_dropshade.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Question_dropshade.png/40px-Question_dropshade.png 2x" width="20"/></a></td>\n<td><b>Unsolved problem in physics</b>:<br/><div style="padding:5px; font-style:italic;">What is dark matter? How is it generated? Is it related to <a href="/wiki/Supersymmetry" title="Supersymmetry">supersymmetry</a>?</div><small><a href="/wiki/List_of_unsolved_problems_in_physics" title="List of unsolved problems in physics">(more unsolved problems in physics)</a></small>\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<table class="wikitable">\n<tbody><tr>\n<th colspan="2">Some dark matter hypotheses<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[82]</a></sup>\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<td rowspan="3">Light bosons\n</td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Quantum_chromodynamics" title="Quantum chromodynamics">quantum chromodynamics</a> <a href="/wiki/Axion" title="Axion">axions</a>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Axion-like_particle&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Axion-like particle (page does not exist)">axion-like particles</a>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Fuzzy_cold_dark_matter" title="Fuzzy cold dark matter">fuzzy cold dark matter</a>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td rowspan="2">neutrinos\n</td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Standard_Model" title="Standard Model">Standard Model</a>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sterile_neutrinos" title="Sterile neutrinos">sterile neutrinos</a>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td rowspan="5"><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Weak_scale&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Weak scale (page does not exist)">weak scale</a>\n</td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Supersymmetry" title="Supersymmetry">supersymmetry</a>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Extra_dimensions" title="Extra dimensions">extra dimensions</a>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Little_Higgs" title="Little Higgs">little Higgs</a>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Effective_field_theory" title="Effective field theory">effective field theory</a>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Simplified_models&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Simplified models (page does not exist)">simplified models</a>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td rowspan="3">other particles\n</td>\n<td><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=WIMPzilla&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="WIMPzilla (page does not exist)">WIMPzilla</a>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Self-interacting_dark_matter" title="Self-interacting dark matter">self-interacting dark matter</a>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Superfluid_vacuum_theory" title="Superfluid vacuum theory">superfluid vacuum theory</a>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td rowspan="3">macroscopic\n</td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Primordial_black_hole" title="Primordial black hole">primordial black holes</a>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Massive_compact_halo_objects" title="Massive compact halo objects">massive compact halo objects</a> (MaCHOs)\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Macroscopic_dark_matter&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Macroscopic dark matter (page does not exist)">Macroscopic dark matter</a> (Macros)\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td rowspan="3"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Modified_gravity" title="Modified gravity">modified gravity</a> (MOG)\n</td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Modified_Newtonian_dynamics" title="Modified Newtonian dynamics">modified Newtonian dynamics</a> (MoND)\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Tensor%E2%80%93vector%E2%80%93scalar_gravity" title="Tensor&#8211;vector&#8211;scalar gravity">Tensor&#8211;vector&#8211;scalar gravity</a> (TeVeS)\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Entropic_gravity" title="Entropic gravity">Entropic gravity</a>\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>Dark matter can refer to any substance that interacts predominantly via gravity with visible matter (e.g., stars and planets). Hence in principle it need not be composed of a new type of fundamental particle but could, at least in part, be made up of standard baryonic matter, such as protons or neutrons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BaryonicFootnote01_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-BaryonicFootnote01-87">[note 4]</a></sup> However, for the reasons outlined below, most scientists think the dark matter is dominated by a non-baryonic component, which is likely composed of a currently unknown fundamental particle (or similar exotic state).\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner"
