Telescope">Fermi-LAT</a> observations of dwarf galaxies provide new insights on dark matter.</div></div></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Baryonic_matter">Baryonic matter</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dark_matter&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Baryonic matter">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Not to be confused with <a href="/wiki/Missing_baryon_problem" title="Missing baryon problem">Missing baryon problem</a>.</div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Baryon" title="Baryon">Baryons</a> (<a href="/wiki/Proton" title="Proton">protons</a> and <a href="/wiki/Neutron" title="Neutron">neutrons</a>) make up ordinary stars and planets. However, baryonic matter also encompasses less common <a href="/wiki/Black_hole" title="Black hole">black holes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Neutron_star" title="Neutron star">neutron stars</a>, faint old <a href="/wiki/White_dwarf" title="White dwarf">white dwarfs</a> and <a href="/wiki/Brown_dwarf" title="Brown dwarf">brown dwarfs</a>, collectively known as <a href="/wiki/Massive_compact_halo_object" title="Massive compact halo object">massive compact halo objects</a> (MACHOs), which can be hard to detect.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERandall2015286_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERandall2015286-88">[84]</a></sup>\n</p><p>However, multiple lines of evidence suggest the majority of dark matter is not made of baryons:\n</p>\n<ul><li>Sufficient diffuse, baryonic gas or dust would be visible when backlit by stars.</li>\n<li>The theory of <a href="/wiki/Big_Bang_nucleosynthesis" title="Big Bang nucleosynthesis">Big Bang nucleosynthesis</a> predicts the observed <a href="/wiki/Abundance_of_the_chemical_elements" title="Abundance of the chemical elements">abundance of the chemical elements</a>. If there are more baryons, then there should also be more helium, lithium and heavier elements synthesized during the Big Bang.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[85]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[86]</a></sup> Agreement with observed abundances requires that baryonic matter makes up between 4&#8211;5% of the universe\'s <a href="/wiki/Friedmann_equations#Density_parameter" title="Friedmann equations">critical density</a>. In contrast, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Large-scale_structure_of_the_universe" title="Large-scale structure of the universe">large-scale structure</a> and other observations indicate that the total matter density is about 30% of the critical density.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-planckesa2015_80-1"><a href="#cite_note-planckesa2015-80">[77]</a></sup></li>\n<li>Astronomical searches for <a href="/wiki/Gravitational_microlensing" title="Gravitational microlensing">gravitational microlensing</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Milky_Way" title="Milky Way">Milky Way</a> found that at most a small fraction of the dark matter may be in dark, compact, conventional objects (MACHOs, etc.); the excluded range of object masses is from half the Earth\'s mass up to 30 solar masses, which covers nearly all the plausible candidates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[87]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[88]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[89]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[90]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[91]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[92]</a></sup></li>\n<li>Detailed analysis of the small irregularities (anisotropies) in the <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_microwave_background" title="Cosmic microwave background">cosmic microwave background</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[93]</a></sup> Observations by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/WMAP" title="WMAP">WMAP</a> and <a href="/wiki/Planck_(spacecraft)" title="Planck (spacecraft)">Planck</a> indicate that around five sixths of the total matter is in a form that interacts significantly with ordinary matter or <a href="/wiki/Photon" title="Photon">photons</a> only through gravitational effects.</li></ul>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Non-baryonic_matter">Non-baryonic matter</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dark_matter&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Non-baryonic matter">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Candidates for non-baryonic dark matter are hypothetical particles such as <a href="/wiki/Axion" title="Axion">axions</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sterile_neutrino" title="Sterile neutrino">sterile neutrinos</a>, <a href="/wiki/Weakly_interacting_massive_particles" title="Weakly interacting massive particles">weakly interacting massive particles</a> (WIMPs), <a href="/wiki/Gravitationally-interacting_massive_particles" title="Gravitationally-interacting massive particles">gravitationally-interacting massive particles</a> (GIMPs), or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Supersymmetric" title="Supersymmetric">supersymmetric</a> particles. The three neutrino types already observed are indeed abundant, and dark, and matter, but because their individual masses&#8212;however uncertain they may be&#8212;are almost certainly tiny, they can only supply a small fraction of dark matter, due to limits derived from <a href="/wiki/Observable_universe" title="Observable universe">large-scale structure</a> and high-<a href="/wiki/Redshift" title="Redshift">redshift</a> galaxies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bertone_merritt_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-bertone_merritt-98">[94]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Unlike baryonic matter, nonbaryonic matter did not contribute to the formation of the <a href="/wiki/Chemical_element" title="Chemical element">elements</a> in the early universe (<a href="/wiki/Big_Bang_nucleosynthesis" title="Big Bang nucleosynthesis">Big Bang nucleosynthesis</a>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Copi_1995_15-2"><a href="#cite_note-Copi_1995-15">[13]</a></sup> and so its presence is revealed only via its gravitational effects, or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Weak_lensing" title="Weak lensing">weak lensing</a>. In addition, if the particles of which it is composed are supersymmetric, they can undergo <a href="/wiki/Annihilation" title="Annihilation">annihilation</a> interactions with themselves, possibly resulting in observable by-products such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gamma_rays" title="Gamma rays">gamma rays</a> and neutrinos (indirect detection).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bertone_merritt_98-1"><a href="#cite_note-bertone_merritt-98">[94]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dark_matter_aggregation_and_dense_dark_matter_objects">Dark matter aggregation and dense dark matter objects</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dark_matter&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Dark matter aggregation and dense dark matter objects">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>If dark matter is as common as observations suggest, an obvious question is whether it can form objects equivalent to <a href="/wiki/Planet" title="Planet">planets</a>, <a href="/wiki/Star" title="Star">stars</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Black_hole" title="Black hole">black holes</a>. The answer has historically been that it cannot,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-curio_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-curio-99">[95]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-siegel_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-siegel-100">[96]</a></sup> because of two factors:\n</p>\n<ul><li><i>It lacks an efficient means to lose energy:</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-curio_99-1"><a href="#cite_note-curio-99">[95]</a></sup> Ordinary matter forms dense objects because it has numerous ways to lose energy. Losing energy would be essential for object formation, because a particle that gains energy during compaction or falling "inward" under gravity, and cannot lose it any other way, will heat up and increase <a href="/wiki/Velocity" title="Velocity">velocity</a> and <a href="/wiki/Momentum" title="Momentum">momentum</a>. Dark matter appears to lack means to lose energy, simply because it is not capable of interacting strongly in other ways except through gravity. The <a href="/wiki/Virial_theorem" title="Virial theorem">virial theorem</a> suggests that such a particle would not stay bound to the gradually forming object&#8212;as the object began to form and compact, the dark matter particles within it would speed up and tend to escape.</li>\n<li><i>It lacks a range of interactions needed to form structures:</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-siegel_100-1"><a href="#cite_note-siegel-100">[96]</a></sup> Ordinary matter interacts in many different ways. This allows the matter to form more complex structures. For example, stars form through gravity, but the particles within them interact and can emit energy in the form of <a href="/wiki/Neutrino" title="Neutrino">neutrinos</a> and <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_radiation" title="Electromagnetic radiation">electromagnetic radiation</a> through <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fusion" title="Nuclear fusion">fusion</a> when they become energetic enough. <a href="/wiki/Proton" title="Proton">Protons</a> and <a href="/wiki/Neutron" title="Neutron">neutrons</a> can bind via the <a href="/wiki/Strong_interaction" title="Strong interaction">strong interaction</a> and then form <a href="/wiki/Atom" title="Atom">atoms</a> with <a href="/wiki/Electron" title="Electron">electrons</a> largely through <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_interaction" title="Electromagnetic interaction">electromagnetic interaction</a>. But there is no evidence that dark matter is capable of such a wide variety of interactions, since it only seems to interact through gravity and through some means no stronger than the <a href="/wiki/Weak_interaction" title="Weak interaction">weak interaction</a> (although this is speculative until dark matter is better understood).</li></ul>\n<p>In 2015&#8211;2017 the idea that dense dark matter was composed of <a href="/wiki/Primordial_black_hole" title="Primordial black hole">primordial black holes</a>, made a comeback<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[97]</a></sup> following results of <a href="/wiki/Gravitational_wave" title="Gravitational wave">gravitation wave</a> measurements which detected the merger of intermediate mass black holes. Black holes with about 30 solar masses are not predicted to form by either stellar collapse (typically less than 15 solar masses) or by the merger of black holes in galactic centers (millions or billions of solar masses). It was proposed that the intermediate mass black holes causing the detected merger formed in the hot dense early phase of the universe due to denser regions collapsing.  However this was later ruled out by a survey of about a thousand supernova which detected no gravitational lensing events, although about 8 would be expected if intermediate mass primordial black holes accounted for the majority of dark matter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[98]</a></sup> The possibility that atom-sized primordial black holes account for a significant fraction of dark matter was ruled out by measurements of positron and electron fluxes outside the suns heliosphere by the Voyager 1 spacecraft. Tiny black holes are theorized to emit <a href="/wiki/Hawking_radiation" title="Hawking radiation">Hawking radiation</a>. However the detected fluxes were too low and did not have the expected energy spectrum suggesting that tiny primordial black holes are not widespread enough to account for dark matter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[99]</a></sup> None-the-less research and theories proposing that dense dark matter account for dark matter continue as of 2018, including approaches to dark matter cooling,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[100]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[101]</a></sup> and the question remains unsettled.\n</p>\n<h2><span id="Classification_of_dark_matter:_cold.2C_warm_or_hot"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Classification_of_dark_matter:_cold,_warm_or_hot">Classification of dark matter: cold, warm or hot</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dark_matter&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Classification of dark matter: cold, warm or hot">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Dark matter can be divided into <i>cold</i>, <i>warm</i>, and <i>hot</i> categories.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[102]</a></sup> These categories refer to velocity rather than an actual temperature, indicating how far corresponding objects moved due to random motions in the early universe, before they slowed due to cosmic expansion&#8212;this is an important distance called the <a href="/wiki/Free_streaming" title="Free streaming">free streaming</a> length (FSL). Primordial density fluctuations smaller than this length get washed out as particles spread from overdense to underdense regions, while larger fluctuations are unaffected; therefore this length sets a minimum scale for later structure formation. The categories are set with respect to the size of a <a href="/wiki/Protogalaxy" title="Protogalaxy">protogalaxy</a> (an object that later evolves into a dwarf galaxy): dark matter particles are classified as cold, warm, or hot according to their FSL; much smaller (cold), similar to (warm), or much larger (hot) than a protogalaxy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[103]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[104]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Mixtures of the above are also possible: a theory of <a href="/wiki/Mixed_dark_matter" title="Mixed dark matter">mixed dark matter</a> was popular in the mid-1990s, but was rejected following the discovery of <a href="/wiki/Dark_energy" title="Dark energy">dark energy</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>\n</p><p>Cold dark matter leads to a bottom-up formation of structure with galaxies forming first and galaxy clusters at a latter stage, while hot dark matter would result in a top-down formation scenario with large matter aggregations forming early, later fragmenting into separate galaxies;<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (September 2018)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup> the latter is excluded by high-redshift galaxy observations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bertone_hooper_silk_16-2"><a href="#cite_note-bertone_hooper_silk-16">[14]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Alternative_definitions">Alternative definitions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dark_matter&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Alternative definitions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>These categories also correspond to <a href="/wiki/Fluctuation_spectrum" title="Fluctuation spectrum">fluctuation spectrum</a> effects and the interval following the Big Bang at which each type became non-relativistic. Davis <i>et al.</i> wrote in 1985:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[105]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303468">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Candidate particles can be grouped into three categories on the basis of their effect on the <a href="/wiki/Mixed_dark_matter" title="Mixed dark matter">fluctuation spectrum</a> (Bond <i>et al.</i> 1983). If the dark matter is composed of abundant light particles which remain relativistic until shortly before recombination, then it may be termed "hot". The best candidate for hot dark matter is a neutrino ... A second possibility is for the dark matter particles to interact more weakly than neutrinos, to be less abundant, and to have a mass of order 1&#160;keV. Such particles are termed "warm dark matter", because they have lower thermal velocities than massive neutrinos ... there are at present few candidate particles which fit this description. <a href="/wiki/Gravitino" title="Gravitino">Gravitinos</a> and <a href="/wiki/Photino" title="Photino">photinos</a> have been suggested (Pagels and Primack 1982; Bond, Szalay and Turner 1982) ... Any particles which became nonrelativistic very early, and so were able to diffuse a negligible distance, are termed "cold" dark matter (CDM). There are many candidates for CDM including supersymmetric particles. </p><div class="templatequotecite">&#8212;&#8201;<cite>M. Davis, G. Efstathiou, C.S. Frenk, and S.D.M. White, The evolution of large-scale structure in a universe dominated by cold dark matter</cite></div>\n</blockquote>\n<p>Another approximate dividing line is that warm dark matter became non-relativistic when the universe was approximately 1 year old and 1 millionth of its present size and in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radiation-dominated_era" title="Radiation-dominated era">radiation-dominated era</a> (photons and neutrinos), with a photon temperature 2.7 million K. Standard physical cosmology gives the <a href="/wiki/Particle_horizon" title="Particle horizon">particle horizon</a> size as 2<i>ct</i> (speed of light multiplied by time) in the radiation-dominated era, thus 2 light-years. A region of this size would expand to 2 million light-years today (absent structure formation). The actual FSL is approximately 5 times the above length, since it continues to grow slowly as particle velocities decrease inversely with the scale factor after they become non-relativistic. In this example the FSL would correspond to 10 million light-years, or 3 mega<a href="/wiki/Parsec" title="Parsec">parsecs</a>, today, around the size containing an average large galaxy.\n</p><p>The 2.7 million K photon temperature gives a typical photon energy of 250 electron-volts, thereby setting a typical mass scale for warm dark matter: particles much more massive than this, such as GeV&#8211;TeV mass <a href="/wiki/Weakly_interacting_massive_particles" title="Weakly interacting massive particles">WIMPs</a>, would become non-relativistic much earlier than one year after the Big Bang and thus have FSLs much smaller than a protogalaxy, making them cold. Conversely, much lighter particles, such as neutrinos with masses of only a few eV, have FSLs much larger than a protogalaxy, thus qualifying them as hot.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cold_dark_matter">Cold dark matter</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dark_matter&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Cold dark matter">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Cold_dark_matter" title="Cold dark matter">Cold dark matter</a></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Cold_dark_matter" title="Cold dark matter">Cold dark matter</a> offers the simplest explanation for most cosmological observations. It is dark matter composed of constituents with an FSL much smaller than a protogalaxy. This is the focus for dark matter research, as hot dark matter does not seem capable of supporting galaxy or galaxy cluster formation, and most particle candidates slowed early.\n</p><p>The constituents of cold dark matter are unknown. Possibilities range from large objects like MACHOs (such as black holes<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hawkins_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hawkins-110">[106]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Preon_star" title="Preon star">Preon stars</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Phys.Lett.B616,1(2005_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-Phys.Lett.B616,1(2005-111">[107]</a></sup>) or <a href="/wiki/Robust_associations_of_massive_baryonic_objects" title="Robust associations of massive baryonic objects">RAMBOs</a> (such as clusters of brown dwarfs), to new particles such as <a href="/wiki/Weakly_interacting_massive_particles" title="Weakly interacting massive particles">WIMPs</a> and <a href="/wiki/Axion" title="Axion">axions</a>.\n</p><p>Studies of <a href="/wiki/Big_Bang_nucleosynthesis" title="Big Bang nucleosynthesis">Big Bang nucleosynthesis</a> and gravitational lensing convinced most cosmologists<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bertone_hooper_silk_16-3"><a href="#cite_note-bertone_hooper_silk-16">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carr_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-Carr-112">[108]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Peter_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-Peter-113">[109]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Garrett_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-Garrett-114">[110]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bertone_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bertone-115">[111]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Olive_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-Olive-116">[112]</a></sup> that MACHOs<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carr_112-1"><a href="#cite_note-Carr-112">[108]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Garrett_114-1"><a href="#cite_note-Garrett-114">[110]</a></sup> cannot make up more than a small fraction of dark matter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Copi_1995_15-3"><a href="#cite_note-Copi_1995-15">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carr_112-2"><a href="#cite_note-Carr-112">[108]</a></sup> According to A. Peter: "... the only <i>really plausible</i> dark-matter candidates are new particles."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Peter_113-1"><a href="#cite_note-Peter-113">[109]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The 1997 <a href="/wiki/DAMA/NaI" title="DAMA/NaI">DAMA/NaI</a> experiment and its successor <a href="/wiki/DAMA/LIBRA" title="DAMA/LIBRA">DAMA/LIBRA</a> in 2013, claimed to directly detect dark matter particles passing through the Earth, but many researchers remain skeptical, as negative results from similar experiments seem incompatible with the DAMA results.\n</p><p>Many <a href="/wiki/Supersymmetry" title="Supersymmetry">supersymmetric</a> models offer dark matter candidates in the form of the WIMPy <a href="/wiki/Lightest_Supersymmetric_Particle" title="Lightest Supersymmetric Particle">Lightest Supersymmetric Particle</a> (LSP).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[113]</a></sup> Separately, heavy sterile neutrinos exist in non-supersymmetric extensions to the <a href="/wiki/Standard_Model" title="Standard Model">standard model</a> that explain the small <a href="/wiki/Neutrino" title="Neutrino">neutrino</a> mass through the <a href="/wiki/Seesaw_mechanism" title="Seesaw mechanism">seesaw mechanism</a>.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Warm_dark_matter">Warm dark matter</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dark_matter&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Warm dark matter">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Warm_dark_matter" title="Warm dark matter">Warm dark matter</a></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Warm_dark_matter" title="Warm dark matter">Warm dark matter</a> comprises particles with an FSL comparable to the size of a protogalaxy. Predictions based on warm dark matter are similar to those for cold dark matter on large scales, but with less small-scale density perturbations. This reduces the predicted abundance of dwarf galaxies and may lead to lower density of dark matter in the central parts of large galaxies. Some researchers consider this a better fit to observations. A challenge for this model is the lack of particle candidates with the required mass ~ 300 eV to 3000 eV.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>\n</p><p>No known particles can be categorized as warm dark matter. A postulated candidate is the <a href="/wiki/Sterile_neutrino" title="Sterile neutrino">sterile neutrino</a>: a heavier, slower form of neutrino that does not interact through the <a href="/wiki/Weak_interaction" title="Weak interaction">weak force</a>, unlike other neutrinos. Some modified gravity theories, such as <a href="/wiki/Scalar%E2%80%93tensor%E2%80%93vector_gravity" title="Scalar&#8211;tensor&#8211;vector gravity">scalar&#8211;tensor&#8211;vector gravity</a>, require "warm" dark matter to make their equations work.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hot_dark_matter">Hot dark matter</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dark_matter&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Hot dark matter">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Hot_dark_matter" title="Hot dark matter">Hot dark matter</a></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Hot_dark_matter" title="Hot dark matter">Hot dark matter</a> consists of particles whose FSL is much larger than the size of a protogalaxy. The <a href="/wiki/Neutrino" title="Neutrino">neutrino</a> qualifies as such particle. They were discovered independently, long before the hunt for dark matter: they were postulated in 1930, and <a href="/wiki/Cowan%E2%80%93Reines_neutrino_experiment" title="Cowan&#8211;Reines neutrino experiment">detected in 1956</a>. Neutrinos\' <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neutrino_mass" title="Neutrino mass">mass</a> is less than 10<sup>&#8722;6</sup> that of an <a href="/wiki/Electron" title="Electron">electron</a>. Neutrinos interact with normal matter only via gravity and the <a href="/wiki/Weak_interaction" title="Weak interaction">weak force</a>, making them difficult to detect (the weak force only works over a small distance, thus a neutrino triggers a weak force event only if it hits a nucleus head-on). This makes them \'weakly interacting light particles\' (WILPs), as opposed to WIMPs.\n</p><p>The three known <a href="/wiki/Flavour_(particle_physics)" title="Flavour (particle physics)">flavours</a> of neutrinos are the <i>electron</i>, <i>muon</i>, and <i>tau</i>. Their masses are slightly different. Neutrinos oscillate among the flavours as they move. It is hard to determine an exact <a href="/wiki/Upper_and_lower_bounds" title="Upper and lower bounds">upper bound</a> on the collective average mass of the three neutrinos (or for any of the three individually). For example, if the average neutrino mass were over 50 <i>eV/c</i><sup>2</sup> (less than 10<sup>&#8722;5</sup> of the mass of an electron), the universe would collapse. CMB data and other methods indicate that their average mass probably does not exceed 0.3 <i>eV/c</i><sup>2</sup>. Thus, observed neutrinos cannot explain dark matter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[114]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Because galaxy-size density fluctuations get washed out by free-streaming, hot dark matter implies that the first objects that can form are huge <a href="/wiki/Supercluster" title="Supercluster">supercluster</a>-size pancakes, which then fragment into galaxies. <a href="/wiki/List_of_deep_fields" title="List of deep fields">Deep-field observations</a> show instead that galaxies formed first, followed by clusters and superclusters as galaxies clump together.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Detection_of_dark_matter_particles">Detection of dark matter particles</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dark_matter&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Detection of dark matter particles">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>If dark matter is made up of sub-atomic particles, then millions, possibly billions, of such particles must pass through every square centimeter of the Earth each second.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gaitskell_2004_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gaitskell_2004-119">[115]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Number_per_second_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-Number_per_second-120">[116]</a></sup> Many experiments aim to test this hypothesis. Although <a href="/wiki/Weakly_interacting_massive_particles" title="Weakly interacting massive particles">WIMPs</a> are popular search candidates,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bertone_hooper_silk_16-4"><a href="#cite_note-bertone_hooper_silk-16">[14]</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Axion_Dark_Matter_Experiment" title="Axion Dark Matter Experiment">Axion Dark Matter Experiment</a> (ADMX) searches for <a href="/wiki/Axion" title="Axion">axions</a>. Another candidate is heavy <a href="/wiki/Hidden_sector" title="Hidden sector">hidden sector</a> particles that only interact with ordinary matter via gravity.\n</p><p>These experiments can be divided into two classes: direct detection experiments, which search for the scattering of dark matter particles off atomic nuclei within a detector; and indirect detection, which look for the products of dark matter particle annihilations or decays.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bertone_merritt_98-2"><a href="#cite_note-bertone_merritt-98">[94]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Direct_detection">Direct detection</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dark_matter&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Direct detection">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Weakly_interacting_massive_particles#Direct_detection" title="Weakly interacting massive particles">Weakly interacting massive particles &#167;&#160;Direct detection</a></div>\n<p>Direct detection experiments aim to observe low-energy recoils (typically a few <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/KeV" title="KeV">keVs</a>) of nuclei induced by interactions with particles of dark matter, which (in theory) are passing through the Earth. After such a recoil the nucleus will emit energy in the form of <a href="/wiki/Scintillation_(physics)" title="Scintillation (physics)">scintillation</a> light or <a href="/wiki/Phonon" title="Phonon">phonons</a>, as they pass through sensitive detection apparatus. To do this effectively, it is crucial to maintain a low background, and so such experiments operate deep underground to reduce the interference from <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_ray" title="Cosmic ray">cosmic rays</a>. Examples of underground laboratories with direct detection experiments include the <a href="/wiki/Stawell_Underground_Physics_Laboratory" title="Stawell Underground Physics Laboratory">Stawell mine</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Soudan_mine" title="Soudan mine">Soudan mine</a>, the <a href="/wiki/SNOLAB" title="SNOLAB">SNOLAB</a> underground laboratory at <a href="/wiki/Greater_Sudbury" title="Greater Sudbury">Sudbury</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gran_Sasso_National_Laboratory" title="Gran Sasso National Laboratory">Gran Sasso National Laboratory</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Canfranc_Underground_Laboratory" title="Canfranc Underground Laboratory">Canfranc Underground Laboratory</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Boulby_Underground_Laboratory" title="Boulby Underground Laboratory">Boulby Underground Laboratory</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Deep_Underground_Science_and_Engineering_Laboratory" title="Deep Underground Science and Engineering Laboratory">Deep Underground Science and Engineering Laboratory</a> and the <a href="/wiki/China_Jinping_Underground_Laboratory" title="China Jinping Underground Laboratory">China Jinping Underground Laboratory</a>.\n</p><p>These experiments mostly use either cryogenic or noble liquid detector technologies. Cryogenic detectors operating at temperatures below 100&#160;mK, detect the heat produced when a particle hits an atom in a crystal absorber such as <a href="/wiki/Germanium" title="Germanium">germanium</a>. <a href="/wiki/Noble_gas" title="Noble gas">Noble liquid</a> detectors detect <a href="/wiki/Scintillation_(physics)" title="Scintillation (physics)">scintillation</a> produced by a particle collision in liquid <a href="/wiki/Xenon" title="Xenon">xenon</a> or <a href="/wiki/Argon" title="Argon">argon</a>. Cryogenic detector experiments include: <a href="/wiki/Cryogenic_Dark_Matter_Search" title="Cryogenic Dark Matter Search">CDMS</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cryogenic_Rare_Event_Search_with_Superconducting_Thermometers" title="Cryogenic Rare Event Search with Superconducting Thermometers">CRESST</a>, <a href="/wiki/EDELWEISS" title="EDELWEISS">EDELWEISS</a>, <a href="/wiki/European_Underground_Rare_Event_Calorimeter_Array" title="European Underground Rare Event Calorimeter Array">EURECA</a>. Noble liquid experiments include ZEPLIN, <a href="/wiki/XENON" title="XENON">XENON</a>, <a href="/wiki/DEAP" title="DEAP">DEAP</a>, <a href="/wiki/ArDM" title="ArDM">ArDM</a>, <a href="/wiki/WIMP_Argon_Programme" title="WIMP Argon Programme">WARP</a>, <a href="/wiki/DarkSide" title="DarkSide">DarkSide</a>, <a href="/wiki/PandaX" title="PandaX">PandaX</a>, and LUX, the <a href="/wiki/Large_Underground_Xenon_experiment" title="Large Underground Xenon experiment">Large Underground Xenon experiment</a>. Both of these techniques focus strongly on their ability to distinguish background particles (which predominantly scatter off electrons) from dark matter particles (that scatter off nuclei). Other experiments include <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/SIMPLE_(dark_matter)" title="SIMPLE (dark matter)">SIMPLE</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PICASSO_(dark_matter)" title="PICASSO (dark matter)">PICASSO</a>.\n</p><p>Currently there has been no well-established claim of dark matter detection from a direct detection experiment, leading instead to strong upper limits on the mass and interaction cross section with nucleons of such dark matter particles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[117]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/DAMA/NaI" title="DAMA/NaI">DAMA/NaI</a> and more recent <a href="/wiki/DAMA/LIBRA" title="DAMA/LIBRA">DAMA/LIBRA</a> experimental collaborations have detected an annual modulation in the rate of events in their detectors,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[118]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[119]</a></sup> which they claim is due to dark matter. This results from the expectation that as the Earth orbits the Sun, the velocity of the detector relative to the <a href="/wiki/Dark_matter_halo" title="Dark matter halo">dark matter halo</a> will vary by a small amount. This claim is so far unconfirmed and in contradiction with negative results from other experiments such as LUX and SuperCDMS.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[120]</a></sup>\n</p><p>A special case of direct detection experiments covers those with directional sensitivity. This is a search strategy based on the motion of the Solar System around the <a href="/wiki/Galactic_Center" title="Galactic Center">Galactic Center</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-apssyn_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-apssyn-125">[121]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-samlee_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-samlee-126">[122]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dmgsheff_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-dmgsheff-127">[123]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kavli_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kavli-128">[124]</a></sup> A low-pressure <a href="/wiki/Time_projection_chamber" title="Time projection chamber">time projection chamber</a> makes it possible to access information on recoiling tracks and constrain WIMP-nucleus kinematics. WIMPs coming from the direction in which the Sun travels (approximately towards <a href="/wiki/Cygnus_(constellation)" title="Cygnus (constellation)">Cygnus</a>) may then be separated from background, which should be isotropic. Directional dark matter experiments include <a href="/wiki/Dark_Matter_Time_Projection_Chamber" title="Dark Matter Time Projection Chamber">DMTPC</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Directional_Recoil_Identification_From_Tracks" title="Directional Recoil Identification From Tracks">DRIFT</a>, Newage and MIMAC.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Indirect_detection">Indirect detection</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dark_matter&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Indirect detection">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Collage_of_six_cluster_collisions_with_dark_matter_maps.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="771" data-file-width="1280" decoding="async" height="133" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Collage_of_six_cluster_collisions_with_dark_matter_maps.jpg/220px-Collage_of_six_cluster_collisions_with_dark_matter_maps.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Collage_of_six_cluster_collisions_with_dark_matter_maps.jpg/330px-Collage_of_six_cluster_collisions_with_dark_matter_maps.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Collage_of_six_cluster_collisions_with_dark_matter_maps.jpg/440px-Collage_of_six_cluster_collisions_with_dark_matter_maps.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Collage_of_six_cluster_collisions_with_dark_matter_maps.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Collage of six cluster collisions with dark matter maps. The clusters were observed in a study of how dark matter in clusters of galaxies behaves when the clusters collide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[125]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><div class="PopUpMediaTransform" id="mwe_player_6" style="width:220px;" videopayload=\'&lt;div class="mediaContainer" style="width:854px"&gt;&lt;video id="mwe_player_7" poster="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Turning_Black_Holes_into_Dark_Matter_Labs.webm/854px--Turning_Black_Holes_into_Dark_Matter_Labs.webm.jpg" controls="" preload="none" autoplay="" style="width:854px;height:480px" class="kskin" data-durationhint="193.323" data-startoffset="0" data-mwtitle="Turning_Black_Holes_into_Dark_Matter_Labs.webm" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons"&gt;&lt;source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/d/d0/Turning_Black_Holes_into_Dark_Matter_Labs.webm/Turning_Black_Holes_into_Dark_Matter_Labs.webm.480p.vp9.webm" type="video/webm; codecs=&amp;quot;vp9, opus&amp;quot;" data-title="Small VP9 (240P)" data-shorttitle="VP9 240P" data-transcodekey="240p.vp9.webm" data-width="426" data-height="240" data-bandwidth="377632" data-framerate="29.97"/&gt;&lt;source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/d/d0/Turning_Black_Holes_into_Dark_Matter_Labs.webm/Turning_Black_Holes_into_Dark_Matter_Labs.webm.360p.webm" type="video/webm; codecs=&amp;quot;vp8, vorbis&amp;quot;" data-title="WebM (360P)" data-shorttitle="WebM 360P" data-transcodekey="360p.webm" data-width="640" data-height="360" data-bandwidth="572488" data-framerate="29.97"/&gt;&lt;source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/d/d0/Turning_Black_Holes_into_Dark_Matter_Labs.webm/Turning_Black_Holes_into_Dark_Matter_Labs.webm.360p.vp9.webm" type="video/webm; codecs=&amp;quot;vp9, opus&amp;quot;" data-title="VP9 (360P)" data-shorttitle="VP9 360P" data-transcodekey="360p.vp9.webm" data-width="640" data-height="360" data-bandwidth="611584" data-framerate="29.97"/&gt;&lt;/video&gt;&lt;/div&gt;\'><img alt="File:Turning Black Holes into Dark Matter Labs.webm" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Turning_Black_Holes_into_Dark_Matter_Labs.webm/220px--Turning_Black_Holes_into_Dark_Matter_Labs.webm.jpg" style="width:220px;height:124px"/><a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/d0/Turning_Black_Holes_into_Dark_Matter_Labs.webm" target="new" title="Play media"><span class="play-btn-large"><span class="mw-tmh-playtext">Play media</span></span></a></div> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Turning_Black_Holes_into_Dark_Matter_Labs.webm" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Video about the potential <a href="/wiki/Gamma-ray_astronomy" title="Gamma-ray astronomy">gamma-ray detection</a> of dark matter <a href="/wiki/Annihilation" title="Annihilation">annihilation</a> around <a href="/wiki/Supermassive_black_hole" title="Supermassive black hole">supermassive black holes</a>. <i>(Duration 3:13, also see file description.)</i></div></div></div>\n<p>Indirect detection experiments search for the products of the self-annihilation or decay of dark matter particles in outer space. For example, in regions of high dark matter density (e.g., the <a href="/wiki/Galactic_Center" title="Galactic Center">centre of our galaxy</a>) two dark matter particles could <a href="/wiki/Annihilation" title="Annihilation">annihilate</a> to produce <a href="/wiki/Gamma_ray" title="Gamma ray">gamma rays</a> or Standard Model particle-antiparticle pairs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bertone2010_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bertone2010-130">[126]</a></sup> Alternatively if the dark matter particle is unstable, it could decay into standard model (or other) particles. These processes could be detected indirectly through an excess of gamma rays, <a href="/wiki/Antiproton" title="Antiproton">antiprotons</a> or <a href="/wiki/Positron" title="Positron">positrons</a> emanating from high density regions in our galaxy or others.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[127]</a></sup> A major difficulty inherent in such searches is that various astrophysical sources can mimic the signal expected from dark matter, and so multiple signals are likely required for a conclusive discovery.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bertone_hooper_silk_16-5"><a href="#cite_note-bertone_hooper_silk-16">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bertone_merritt_98-3"><a href="#cite_note-bertone_merritt-98">[94]</a></sup>\n</p><p>A few of the dark matter particles passing through the Sun or Earth may scatter off atoms and lose energy. Thus dark matter may accumulate at the center of these bodies, increasing the chance of collision/annihilation. This could produce a distinctive signal in the form of high-energy <a href="/wiki/Neutrino" title="Neutrino">neutrinos</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[128]</a></sup> Such a signal would be strong indirect proof of WIMP dark matter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bertone_hooper_silk_16-6"><a href="#cite_note-bertone_hooper_silk-16">[14]</a></sup> High-energy neutrino telescopes such as <a href="/wiki/Antarctic_Muon_And_Neutrino_Detector_Array" title="Antarctic Muon And Neutrino Detector Array">AMANDA</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/IceCube" title="IceCube">IceCube</a> and <a href="/wiki/ANTARES_(telescope)" title="ANTARES (telescope)">ANTARES</a> are searching for this signal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERandall2015298_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERandall2015298-133">[129]</a></sup>\nThe detection by <a href="/wiki/LIGO" title="LIGO">LIGO</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/GW150914" title="GW150914">September 2015</a> of gravitational waves, opens the possibility of observing dark matter in a new way, particularly if it is in the form of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Primordial_black_holes" title="Primordial black holes">primordial black holes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[130]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[131]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[132]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Many experimental searches have been undertaken to look for such emission from dark matter annihilation or decay, examples of which follow.\nThe <a href="/wiki/Energetic_Gamma_Ray_Experiment_Telescope" title="Energetic Gamma Ray Experiment Telescope">Energetic Gamma Ray Experiment Telescope</a> observed more gamma rays in 2008 than expected from the <a href="/wiki/Milky_Way" title="Milky Way">Milky Way</a>, but scientists concluded that this was most likely due to incorrect estimation of the telescope\'s sensitivity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[133]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Fermi_Gamma-ray_Space_Telescope" title="Fermi Gamma-ray Space Telescope">Fermi Gamma-ray Space Telescope</a> is searching for similar gamma rays.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[134]</a></sup> In April 2012, an analysis of previously available data from its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fermi_LAT" title="Fermi LAT">Large Area Telescope</a> instrument produced statistical evidence of a 130 GeV signal in the gamma radiation coming from the center of the Milky Way.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[135]</a></sup> WIMP annihilation was seen as the most probable explanation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[136]</a></sup>\n</p><p>At higher energies, <a href="/wiki/IACT" title="IACT">ground-based gamma-ray telescopes</a> have set limits on the annihilation of dark matter in dwarf <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Spheroidal_galaxy&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Spheroidal galaxy (page does not exist)">spheroidal galaxies</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[137]</a></sup> and in clusters of galaxies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[138]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Payload_for_Antimatter_Matter_Exploration_and_Light-nuclei_Astrophysics" title="Payload for Antimatter Matter Exploration and Light-nuclei Astrophysics">PAMELA</a> experiment (launched in 2006) detected excess <a href="/wiki/Positron" title="Positron">positrons</a>. They could be from dark matter annihilation or from <a href="/wiki/Pulsar" title="Pulsar">pulsars</a>. No excess <a href="/wiki/Antiproton" title="Antiproton">antiprotons</a> were observed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[139]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 2013 results from the <a href="/wiki/Alpha_Magnetic_Spectrometer" title="Alpha Magnetic Spectrometer">Alpha Magnetic Spectrometer</a> on the <a href="/wiki/International_Space_Station" title="International Space Station">International Space Station</a> indicated excess high-energy <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_ray" title="Cosmic ray">cosmic rays</a> that could be due to dark matter annihilation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-APS-20130403_144-0"><a href="#cite_note-APS-20130403-144">[140]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AMS-20130403_145-0"><a href="#cite_note-AMS-20130403-145">[141]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AP-20130403_146-0"><a href="#cite_note-AP-20130403-146">[142]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBC-20130403_147-0"><a href="#cite_note-BBC-20130403-147">[143]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20130403_148-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20130403-148">[144]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20130403_149-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20130403-149">[145]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Collider_searches_for_dark_matter">Collider searches for dark matter</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dark_matter&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Collider searches for dark matter">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>An alternative approach to the detection of dark matter particles in nature is to produce them in a laboratory. Experiments with the <a href="/wiki/Large_Hadron_Collider" title="Large Hadron Collider">Large Hadron Collider</a> (LHC) may be able to detect dark matter particles produced in collisions of the LHC <a href="/wiki/Proton" title="Proton">proton</a> beams. Because a dark matter particle should have negligible interactions with normal visible matter, it may be detected indirectly as (large amounts of) missing energy and momentum that escape the detectors, provided other (non-negligible) collision products are detected.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kane_watson_150-0"><a href="#cite_note-kane_watson-150">[146]</a></sup>\nConstraints on dark matter also exist from the <a href="/wiki/Large_Electron%E2%80%93Positron_Collider" title="Large Electron&#8211;Positron Collider">LEP</a> experiment using a similar principle, but probing the interaction of dark matter particles with electrons rather than quarks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[147]</a></sup> It is important to note that any discovery from collider searches must be corroborated by discoveries in the indirect or direct detection sectors to prove that the particle discovered is, in fact, dark matter.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Alternative_hypotheses">Alternative hypotheses</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dark_matter&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Alternative hypotheses">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Alternatives_to_general_relativity" title="Alternatives to general relativity">Alternatives to general relativity</a></div>\n<p>Because dark matter remains to be conclusively identified, many other hypotheses have emerged aiming to explain the observational phenomena that dark matter was conceived to explain. The most common method is to modify general relativity. General relativity is well-tested on solar system scales, but its validity on galactic or cosmological scales has not been well proven. A suitable modification to general relativity can conceivably eliminate the need for dark matter. The best-known theories of this class are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/MOND" title="MOND">MOND</a> and its relativistic generalization <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tensor-vector-scalar_gravity" title="Tensor-vector-scalar gravity">tensor-vector-scalar gravity</a> (TeVeS),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[148]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/F(R)_gravity" title="F(R) gravity">f(R) gravity</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[149]</a></sup>, the <a href="/wiki/Negative_mass" title="Negative mass">negative mass</a> <a href="/wiki/Dark_fluid" title="Dark fluid">dark fluid</a> proposed by <a href="/wiki/Jamie_Farnes" title="Jamie Farnes">Jamie Farnes</a> from the University of Oxford,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[150]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[151]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Farnes_156-0"><a href="#cite_note-Farnes-156">[152]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Entropic_gravity" title="Entropic gravity">entropic gravity</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-physorgnewtheory_157-0"><a href="#cite_note-physorgnewtheory-157">[153]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Alternatives_to_general_relativity" title="Alternatives to general relativity">Alternative theories</a> abound.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[154]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[155]</a></sup>\n</p><p>A problem with alternative hypotheses is that the observational evidence for dark matter comes from so many independent approaches (see the "observational evidence" section above). Explaining any individual observation is possible but explaining all of them is very difficult. Nonetheless, there have been some scattered successes for alternative hypotheses, such as a 2016 test of gravitational lensing in entropic gravity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[156]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161"><a href="#cite_note-161">[157]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[158]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The prevailing opinion among most astrophysicists is that while modifications to general relativity can conceivably explain part of the observational evidence, there is probably enough data to conclude there must be some form of dark matter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[159]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="In_philosophy_of_science">In philosophy of science</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dark_matter&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: In philosophy of science">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>In <a href="/wiki/Philosophy_of_science" title="Philosophy of science">philosophy of science</a>, dark matter is an example of an auxiliary hypothesis, an <a href="/wiki/Ad_hoc_hypothesis" title="Ad hoc hypothesis">ad hoc</a> postulate that is added to a theory in response to observations that <a href="/wiki/Falsifiability" title="Falsifiability">falsify</a> it. It has been argued that the dark matter hypothesis is a <a href="/wiki/Conventionalism#Epistemology" title="Conventionalism">conventionalist</a> hypothesis, that is, a hypothesis that adds no empirical content and hence is unfalsifiable in the sense defined by <a href="/wiki/Karl_Popper" title="Karl Popper">Karl Popper</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[160]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="In_popular_culture">In popular culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dark_matter&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: In popular culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Dark_matter_in_fiction" title="Dark matter in fiction">Dark matter in fiction</a></div>\n<p>Mention of dark matter is made in works of fiction. In such cases, it is usually attributed extraordinary physical or magical properties. Such descriptions are often inconsistent with the hypothesized properties of dark matter in physics and cosmology.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dark_matter&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r853265166">.mw-parser-output div.columns-2 div.column{float:left;width:50%;min-width:300px}.mw-parser-output div.columns-3 div.column{float:left;width:33.3%;min-width:200px}.mw-parser-output div.columns-4 div.column{float:left;width:25%;min-width:150px}.mw-parser-output div.columns-5 div.column{float:left;width:20%;min-width:120px}</style><div class="columns-2" style="background-color: transparent; width: 100%"><div class="column">\n<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r853265166" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/></div><div class="column">\n<dl><dt>Related theories</dt></dl>\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Dark_energy" title="Dark energy"> Dark energy</a>&#160;&#8211; An unknown physical entity (lato sensu) that causes the expansion of the universe to accelerate. It might be a completely new component of physics or a broader extension of known physics, for example, gravity.</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Conformal_gravity" title="Conformal gravity"> Conformal gravity</a>&#160;&#8211; Gravity theories that are invariant under Weyl transformations</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Density_wave_theory" title="Density wave theory">Density wave theory</a> &#8211; A theory that waves of compressed gas, which move slower than the galaxy, maintains galaxies\' structure</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Entropic_gravity" title="Entropic gravity"> Entropic gravity</a>&#160;&#8211; A theory in modern physics that describes gravity as an entropic force</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Dark_radiation" title="Dark radiation"> Dark radiation</a>&#160;&#8211; A postulated type of radiation that mediates interactions of dark matter</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Massive_gravity" title="Massive gravity"> Massive gravity</a>&#160;&#8211; Theory of gravity in which the graviton has nonzero mass</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Unparticle_physics" title="Unparticle physics"> Unparticle physics</a>&#160;&#8211; A speculative theory that conjectures a form of matter that cannot be explained in terms of particles</li></ul>\n<dl><dt>Experiments</dt></dl>\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/DEAP" title="DEAP"> DEAP</a>, a search apparatus</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Dark_Matter_Particle_Explorer" title="Dark Matter Particle Explorer"> Dark Matter Particle Explorer</a>&#160;(DAMPE), a space mission</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/General_antiparticle_spectrometer" title="General antiparticle spectrometer"> General antiparticle spectrometer</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/MultiDark" title="MultiDark"> MultiDark</a>, a research program</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Illustris_project" title="Illustris project"> Illustris project</a>, astrophysical simulations</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Future_Circular_Collider" title="Future Circular Collider"> Future Circular Collider</a>, a particle accelerator research infrastructure</li></ul>\n<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r853265166" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/></div><div class="column">\n<dl><dt>Dark matter candidates</dt></dl>\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Light_dark_matter" title="Light dark matter"> Light dark matter</a>&#160;&#8211; Dark matter weakly interacting massive particles candidates with masses less than 1 GeV</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Mirror_matter" title="Mirror matter"> Mirror matter</a>&#160;&#8211; A hypothetical counterpart to ordinary matter</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Exotic_matter" title="Exotic matter"> Exotic matter</a>&#160;&#8211; Any kind of non-baryonic matter</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Neutralino#Relationship_to_dark_matter" title="Neutralino"> Neutralino</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Dark_galaxy" title="Dark galaxy"> Dark galaxy</a>&#160;&#8211; A hypothesized galaxy with no, or very few, stars</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Scalar_field_dark_matter" title="Scalar field dark matter"> Scalar field dark matter</a>&#160;&#8211; A classical, minimally coupled, scalar field postulated to account for the inferred dark matter</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Self-interacting_dark_matter" title="Self-interacting dark matter"> Self-interacting dark matter</a>&#160;&#8211; A hypothetical form of dark matter consisting of particles with strong self-interactions</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Weakly_interacting_massive_particles" title="Weakly interacting massive particles"> Weakly interacting massive particles</a>&#160;(WIMP)&#160;&#8211; Hypothetical particles that are thought to constitute dark matter</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Strongly_interacting_massive_particle" title="Strongly interacting massive particle"> Strongly interacting massive particle</a>&#160;(SIMP)&#160;&#8211; Hypothetical particles that interact strongly with ordinary matter, but could form the inferred dark matter despite this</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Chameleon_particle" title="Chameleon particle"> Chameleon particle</a>&#160;&#8211; Hypothetical scalar particle that couples to matter more weakly than gravity</li></ul>\n</div><div style="clear: both"></div></div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dark_matter&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: decimal;">\n<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">\n<li id="cite_note-1"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-1">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">A small portion of dark matter could be baryonic. See <a href="/wiki/Baryonic_dark_matter" title="Baryonic dark matter">Baryonic dark matter</a>.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-10"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-10">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Since dark energy does not count as matter, this is 26.8/(4.9 + 26.8)=0.845</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-51"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-51">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">This is a consequence of the <a href="/wiki/Shell_theorem" title="Shell theorem">shell theorem</a> and the observation that spiral galaxies are spherically symmetric to a large extent (in 2D).</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-BaryonicFootnote01-87"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-BaryonicFootnote01_87-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Astronomers define the term <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Baryonic_matter" title="Baryonic matter">baryonic matter</a> to refer to ordinary matter made of <a href="/wiki/Proton" title="Proton">protons</a>, <a href="/wiki/Neutron" title="Neutron">neutrons</a> and <a href="/wiki/Electron" title="Electron">electrons</a>, including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neutron_stars" title="Neutron stars">neutron stars</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Black_holes" title="Black holes">black holes</a> from the collapse of ordinary matter.  Strictly speaking, electrons are <a href="/wiki/Lepton" title="Lepton">leptons</a> not <a href="/wiki/Baryon" title="Baryon">baryons</a>; but since their number is equal to the protons while their mass is far smaller, electrons give a negligible contribution to the average density of baryonic matter. Baryonic matter excludes other known particles such as <a href="/wiki/Photon" title="Photon">photons</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neutrinos" title="Neutrinos">neutrinos</a>. Hypothetical primordial black holes are also generally defined as non-baryonic, since they would have formed from radiation, not matter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BaryonicSource01_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-BaryonicSource01-86">[83]</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n</ol></div></div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span Difranzo, Anthony (1 February 2018). <a class="external text" href="http://curious.astro.cornell.edu/about-us/95-the-universe/galaxies/general-questions/508-are-there-any-dark-stars-or-dark-galaxies-made-of-dark-matter-advanced" rel="nofollow">"Synopsis: A Way to Cool Dark Matter"</a>. <i>Physical Review Letters</i>. <b>120</b> (5). <a href="/wiki/Digital_object_identifier" title="Digital object identifier">doi</a>:<a class="external text" href="//doi.org/10.1103%2FPhysRevLett.120.051102" rel="nofollow">10.1103/PhysRevLett.120.051102</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PubMed_Identifier" title="PubMed Identifier">PMID</a>&#160;<a class="external text" href="//www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/29481169" rel="nofollow">29481169</a>. <q>One widely held belief about dark matter is that it cannot cool off by radiating energy. If it could, then it might bunch together and create compact objects in the same way that baryonic matter forms planets, stars, and galaxies. Observations so far suggest that dark matter doesn\'t do that&#8212;it resides only in diffuse halos ... As a result, it is extremely unlikely there are very dense objects like stars made out of entirely (or even mostly) dark matter.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Physical+Review+Letters&amp;rft.atitle=Synopsis%3A+A+Way+to+Cool+Dark+Matter&amp;rft.volume=120&amp;rft.issue=5&amp;rft.date=2018-02-01&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1103%2FPhysRevLett.120.051102&amp;rft_id=info%3Apmid%2F29481169&amp;rft.aulast=Buckley&amp;rft.aufirst=Matthew+R.&amp;rft.au=Difranzo%2C+Anthony&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fcurious.astro.cornell.edu%2Fabout-us%2F95-the-universe%2Fgalaxies%2Fgeneral-questions%2F508-are-there-any-dark-stars-or-dark-galaxies-made-of-dark-matter-advanced&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ADark+matter"></span><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r879151008" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-siegel-100"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-siegel_100-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-siegel_100-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/Ethan_Siegel" title="Ethan Siegel">Ethan Siegel</a> (28 October 2013). <a class="external text" href="https://www.forbes.com/sites/startswithabang/2016/10/28/why-doesnt-dark-matter-form-black-holes/#4e5014943de1" rel="nofollow">"Why Doesn\'t Dark Matter Form Black Holes?"</a>. <i>Forbes</i>.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-101"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-101">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation journal">Cho, Adrian (9 February 2017). Johns Hopkins University. 15 June 2016<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">20 June</span> 2015</span>. <q>While their existence has not been established with certainty, primordial black holes have in the past been suggested as a possible solution to the dark matter mystery. Because there&#8217;s so little evidence of them, though, the primordial black hole-dark matter hypothesis has not gained a large following among scientists. The LIGO findings, however, raise the prospect anew, especially as the objects detected in that experiment conform to the mass predicted for dark matter. Predictions made by scientists in the past held that conditions at the birth of the universe would produce lots of these primordial black holes distributed approximately evenly in the universe, clustering in halos around galaxies. All this would make them good candidates for dark matter.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.btitle=Did+Gravitational+Wave+Detector+Find+Dark+Matter%3F&amp;rft.pub=Johns+Hopkins+University&amp;rft.date=2016-06-15&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Freleases.jhu.edu%2F2016%2F06%2F15%2Fdid-gravitational-wave-detector-find-dark-matter%2F&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ADark+matter"></span><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r879151008" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-136"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-136">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation journal">Bird, Simeon; Cholis, Illian (2016).
